Importance and ThreatsSection G
Describe the overallsignificanceand importance of the property to be acquired:
This projectisimportant for publicsafety. Under publicownership, the property willbe managed to
decrease risksfrom wildfire,erosion and flooding and willensure long term health of the watersheds,
wetlands, and coastalmarine areas.Ifthe property Issold to another privateowner, there isno way to
ensure future stewardship and management activitieswillalignwith the community vision.State
acquisitionand co-stewardship with community groups willguarantee that the scenicopen space
property near major population centers and touristattractionswillforever remain a source of
inspirationand treasured community resource foropen space, recreation,and agriculture.
Protecting the coastal plainfrom development has been a community priorityfor decades. There isa
willingsellerlandowner, broad support for acquisition,and Interagency plans for ongoing stewardship.
The Pohakea Watershed Lands are located in a highlyvisiblescenic area at the base of the West Maui
Mountains. The property isa keystone property for watershed protection,provides important natural
habitatand iscriticalfor managing wild fires- a chronicthreatinthisarea of Maui - and for mitigating
erosion,soilrunoff,and pollutionof Ma'aiaea Bay and itsnearshore coralreefs.Purchase of the
property includesthree wells,a 750,000-gallontank and easements for the deliveryof water across
adjacent lands.The water resources willprovide significantbenefitsfor land management that willhelp
prevent erosion that damages marine environments.
Acquisitionwillmaximize publicbenefitsby enabling largescaleprotectionof important naturaland
cultural resources and minimize risk from environmental hazards and naturaldisasters.The property is
ina chronicallywildfireprone area and there Isan urgent need to acquireand manage itfor public
safety.Recent fireshave caused closureof the only road availableforemergency vehiclesneeding to
get to urgent care servicesfrom Lahalna.
DLNR DOFAW already has firebreakson thisproperty,and would incorporate the property in an area
wide wildfiremanagement strategy.Controlof wildfireswould also substantiallycontributeto reducing
erosion and runoff into streams, Pohakea Gulch, and Ma'aiaea Bay,which smothers and damages
nearshore corals.The property willbe part of a mauka to makai "ConservationCorridor"linking
protecting State owned upland watershed lands and Pohakea Gulch to the Kealia National Wildlife
Refuge wetland ponds, coralreefs,and offshore waters. The greaterarea of Ma'aiaea Bay isdedicated
as a NationalHumpback Whale Marine Sanctuary.The property provides exceptionalviews of the West
Maui Mountains to thousands of dallycommuters and thisacquisitionwillprotectregionallyimportant
open space.The historicLahaina PaliTrailruns through the land and there islimitedpublicaccess.Public
accesswillcontinue and acquisitionof the propertywillallowforthe longterm possibilityforenhanced
outdoor recreationand enjoyment, such as improved trailheadparkingiffuturecommunity based
management planningindicatesthere iscommunity need/desirefor such improvements.
The Divisionof Forestryand Wildlife(DOFAW) iscurrentlyengaged In a broad collaborativeeffortwith
agency, NGO, and landowner partnersto plan and implement land management solutionsto break the
fireand erosioncycles,restorehabitats,and stoperosionthatfoulsmarine and nearshoreecosystems in
south and west Maui. Those plans includeset aside of the mauka statelands to forestreserve and
incorporationIntothe Mauna Kahalawai Watershed Partnership,enabling DOFAW and partners to
directadditionaloperational resources to effectivemanagement. Acquisitionof the subject parcel by
conservation partners with management capacityIsa key component of thiseffortbecause, 1) a
number of the most damaging sources of erosion are located on the parceland must be addressed on
that land and, 2) the parcel liesbetween the highway and the mauka state lands and therefore holds the
potential,through maintenance of habitat and firebreaks,of preventing the movement of fires,both
from the highway mauka and from the mauka lands across the highway to the community. Acquisition
of the subject parcelwillenable DOFAW to place itintothe forestreserve system, providingconnectivity
of landsand continuityof conservationaction.Without those efforts,the parcelwilllikelycontinue to be
threatened by wildfireand erosion and the cyclewillpersist.Vegetation willbecome overgrown and fuel
wildfires.Furthermore, ifthe current landowners subdividesthe property for a housing development or
sellsthe property to another privatedeveloper,the landwillcontinueto losefertiletop soilviaerosion,
flooding,and stormwater runoff.Muddy runoffwilldamage the marine ecosystem, inhibituse of
nearshore waters for recreational,subsistence,and culturaluse,and threaten ocean- and recreation-
based businesses.Recovering lossesfrom wildfires,erosion,and reefdamage comes at significant
expense to taxpayersand some lossesare irrevocable.Thispropertyisimportantas itisa key pieceof
area-wide effortsto manage mauka lands ina responsibleway thatprotectsmakai water quality.
Stateownership willprotectthese unique and threatened naturaland culturalresourcesby
safeguardingculturaland historicarchaeologicalsitesand providegreen infrastructureto reduce
negativeimpactscaused by erosionand stormwater runoff.The propertyIsinthevicinityoflandswith
richculturaland archaeological sitesand itisimportant to preservewhat remainson an area prizedfor
itsiconicscenicviews and unique aestheticresources.
Identifyand assessconditionsthatthreatenthe significanceand Importanceofthe property.Address,
where applicable,erosion,sedimentation,pollutedrunoff,flooding,invasivespecies,conflicting
activities:
The subjectparceland the 3,400acresofunencumbered statelandsmauka oftheparcelareproneto
hazardous cyclesof wildfiresfollowed by massive erosionevents.The firesthreatenpublicsafetyand
propertiesinthe nearby commercial and residentialareasand the subsequentsoillossthreatensa
significantwatershed and smother nearshorecoralreefecosystemsthatsupportrecreational,
commercial, and subsistence activities,resultinginsignificantdamage to those ecosystems
The stateand privatelandsinthe Pohakea Watershed of Ma'alaeapose importantchallengesfor
resourcemanagers.The dry and windy conditionsofthearea,coupledwithan abundanceofvegetation
present on the subjectparcelare highlyconducive tothe rapidspreadofwildfires.The propertyisa
major drainageway forwatersfunnelingdown from the West Maui MountainstoPohakeaGulch.The
property currentlyhas inadequate stormwater management and thereisan urgentneed to manage
vegetationto reduce fuelsforwildlandfiresand to slow erosionand siltladenrunofffrom entering
Ma'alaea Bay,which Iscurrentlylistedas an impairedwater body.
As a percentoftotallandarea,Hawaii'swildfiresburnasmuch ormore landeachyearthananyother
state.Wildfiresaffectmental health, degrade airqualityand harm coralreefs,threatenfreshwater
suppliesand detertourists.Catastrophicwildfirestakea largeeconomictollon communitiesthrough
propertylosses,decreased tourism,and even changes Inthelong-termstructureofthelocaleconomy.
SignificantbrushfiresinMa'alaea have occurred recentlyon averageeverytwo-yearsand spread
towards West Maui binging a significantexpense to taxpayersand greatrisktofirefighters.Climate
predictionsand changing weather patternsin Hawaii suggestthatinvasivegrassesand shrubswillsee
stronggrowth and createa self-replenishingfuelthatoutpaces nativevegetation.Highertemperatures
and drierconditionscontributed to the July2019 wildfirethat burned 9,000 acres and the October 2019
wildfirethat burned over 4,500 in Ma'alaea and Pohakea. Thousands of residentsand touristsevacuated
towns on the west coast,two major roads were closed while firedepartment crews builtup a berm to
prevent the firefrom encroaching onto the highway, and numerous flightswere diverteddue to smoke.
Preventing development on thisscenic property has been a longstanding community priorityand there
continues to be realthreat that the property could become another dense agriculturalsubdivision.The
landowner has been approached many times with offersto purchase the property and there are no
guarantees that a future owner would retainthe scenicopen space that isbeloved by the community.
Priorsubdivisionproposals have been a few votes shy of gettingfullapproval and future attempts to
develop the property may be granted by current or future decisionmakers. Publicacquisitionisurgently
needed to ensure that a privatebuyer without any stewardship obligationsdoes not furthercontribute
to environmental hazards exacerbated by lackof management.

DOFAW, Maui FireDepartment (MFD), Maui Nul Marine Resource Council (MNMRC), the Pohakea

Set aside of thisproperty and adjacent State owned lands for inclusionin the West Maui Forest Reserve
willplace them under Chapter 13-104, HAR, which provides legalprotectionsfortheirnaturaland
culturalresourcevalues.DLNR/DOFAW willmanage the property holisticallyto protect,restore,monitor
natural resources, and provide recreationalopportunities,aesthetic benefits,watershed restoration,
habitatmanagement, and other community benefits.
The State plansto continue working with allthe partners- HECO/MECO, Hut o Na Wai 'Eha,Ma'alaea
VillageAssociation,Maui FireDepartment, Maui CulturalLands, Maui Nui Marine Resources Council,
Mauna Kahalawai Watershed Partnership,and others-to prevent wildfire,reduce erosion and runoff,
and improve nearshore water qualityat Ma'alaea Bay. Management actions include construction and
maintenance of firebreaks, habitat restoration,construction of erosion control measures to include soil
stabilizationand capture, and establishment of a seriesof green breaks to reduce firespread.
● Resource management plan
There are a number of land management plans availablethat address resource conservation, erosion,
and fireneeds for the subject parceland adjacent lands. Each iscurrentlybeing implemented by
agency, community, and landowner partners. Those plans include:
1) The Pohakea Watershed Stormwater Management Plan,which provides documentation of the
sources, locations,and causes of erosion and sediment transport caused by surface water flow
contributingto water qualitydegradation indownstream near shore and marine habitats.The
plan provides source by source recommendations for actions needed to reduce erosion and
impacts to marine habitats.
2) The Mauna Kahalawai Watershed Management Plan,a comprehensive management plan to
protect and preserve nativeecosystems and fresh water supply forthe 50,000-acre watershed
landsthat comprise Mauna Kahalawai Watershed Partnership.The plan Includesdetailed
guidance forthe controlof feralungulates (wildhooved animals) and invasivespecies,
monitoring threatsand performance toward management objectives,and education and
outreach to engage communities in naturalresourcesprotection.
Community WildfirePlans:The Western Maui Community WildfireProtectionPlanand the
South Maui Community WildfireProtectionPlan callfor numerous management actionsto
prevent and suppress wildfires,controland mitigateerosion,restoreand reforesthabitat,
provideforcompatible publicuse,and fostercommunity engagement. DOFAW isworking with
Hawaiian ElectricCompany to plan and implement measures to reduce ignitionfrom power lines
suspectedto be a source of ignitionon landsadjacentto the subjectproperty.

● Management entity(identity,expertise,experience)
A number of community organizations are interested in co-management with the State of the biological
and culturalresources of the property. Some entitiesare already involved in management of the
property and nearby lands,and others are poised to activelyparticipate.DLNR/DOFAW willbe the
primary management entityand has considerable expertiseand experience with land stewardship and
management.
DOFAW maintains approximately 18 miles of firebreaks inWest Maui, includingseveralmiles of breaks
on the mauka lands of the Pohakea Watershed. In 2020, DOFAW and the Maui Nui Marine Resources
Council partnered to expand firebreakwork to the subject parcel,creating an additional3.5 miles of fire
breaks along the length of the parceladjacent to the highway makai and to the state lands mauka.
DOFAW plans to expand those firebreaksby adding three connecting cross breaks to the parcel,
includinga number of green breaks,for a totalof 4.5 miles of breaks on the parcel.

property

stillan ahupua'a boundary between Ukumehame and Wailuku lengthwise.The property isadjacentto

SECTION I. CULTURAL AND HISTORICAL SIGNIFICANCE
Completed Section I attachedon next page25.
15
Cultural and HistoricSignificanceSection I:
Describe the culturalsignificanceand historicuses of the land to be acquired, including:
● Place names, with translationsand possibleinterpretations
● Known pre-contact history and land uses
● Information about neighboring areas, land divisions,and management units,in
relationshipto the land to be acquired
Ma'alaea, being a possiblecontractionof the word Maka 'alaea(ocherous earth beginning)isan
ahupua'a (landdivisionfrom the mountain to the sea)Inthe Wailuku District.Ma'alaea isrichincultural
and naturalresources that play a significantrolein inthe health and lifeof the land and itspeoples.The
Ma'alaea Mauka property consistsof 257 acres of dry land conditionsthat isnestled between the slopes
of Mauna Kahalawai to the west and Honoapi'ilaniHighway to the east.
A number of perennial streams flow through the lands of Ma'alaea from both directionsof East and
West Maui. One of the most famous of these streams isWaikapu stream, which ispart of the largerNa
Wai 'Eha or "The Four Waters," which consistof Waikapu, Wailuku, Waiehu, and Waihe'e. The makai
portionsof Ma'alaea consistsof two coastalfeatures,KealiaPond and the famous Ma'alaea Bay.
Kealia Pond isan aquatic habitat for various native marine lifesuch as 'aholehole, 'ama'ama, and the
endangered 'opae 'ula.Itisalso the home for several endangered nativewater birdssuch as ae'o,'alae
'ula,and 'auku'u.Currently,KealiaPond isconsidered a NationalWildlifeRefuge.
Ma'alaea Bay isalsoa culturaland modern day resource.Recreationally,Ma'alaea Bay providesintense
surfingconditionsforthe most skilledsurfersdue to the reefbreak as well as the mauka wind patterns.
The extensivereefsystem along the bay acted as a vitalsubsistencefisheryforfishermen as well as a
sanctuaryfor many nativespeciesoffish.The reefalsoacted as an 'ilinaor burialground where many
Native Hawaiians would bury the bones of theirancestors inthe reef.Ma'alaea Bay also has a rich
historyfor itsuse as a prime transitarea fortravelduringboth pre-contactand post-contactperiod.
Afterthe passingof ChiefKekaulike,a canoe landed in Ma'alaea Bay that contained the chiefs remains.
Priorto 1945, Ma'alaea Bay was used by the U.S.marine divisionas a practicearea and by the mid-
1950s, constructionof the Ma'alaea harbor began.
Protectingthe Ma'alaea Mauka property would help preservea varietyof culturalcomplexes and
archaeologicalfeatures such as pohaku (stones;Site1286, Site1440),seventeen habitationcomplexes
(Site1287),petroglyphs(Site1169),includingthe entrance portion of Lahaina PaliTrail,which traverses
across the State-protectedconservations lands.Lahaina PaliTrail(StateHistoricsitenumber 0-50-09-
2946) ishome to approximately 18 archaeologicalsitesthat are locatedinthe surrounding area.These
landsare vitalforprotectionas runofffrom heavy rainsfrom these landscan potentiallyaffectother
culturalresources makai of thisproperty.

26. Identifyand explainhow theproposed acquisitionwould assure the long-term protection
of the land and itsresourcevalues in a way thatisappropriatefortheirintendeduse,and
would ensure that land is nor used in a manner that isinconsistentwith thepurposes for
which itwas acquired.For example, summarize theobjectives,terms,and conditionsof a
proposed conservationeasement,agriculturaleasement,deed restrictions,and covenants.
The threatsposed by thehighlydamaging cyclesof fireand erosiontothesubjectparcelare
sharedby thelandsthelandsmauka of the subjectparcel,which includethePohakea Watershed
(TMKs (2)3-6-001:014and 4-8-001:001).By theirconnectivity,soils,topography,and flashy
fuels,the fateof each istiedto the other. The acquisitionproposedhere seekstoaddressthose
issues,as partof a broadercollaborativeeffortwith conservationpartners,throughthesetaside
and incorporationoftheunencumbered mauka landsand thesubjectparceltotheforestreserve
system. SetasideofthoselandsforinclusionintheWest Maui ForestReservewillplacethem
under Chapter13-104,Hawaii AdministrativeRules,which provideslegalprotectionsforthe
land’snaturalresourcevalues.ForestReserve designationwillalsoenhance stewardshipand
management opportunitiesby placingthem underthemanagement ofDOF AW withbudgeted
supportforresourcemanagement, includingdevelopmentand implementationoffire
presuppressionand suppressionplans.Inaddition,we plantoincludethelandsintheMauna
Kahalawai Watershed Partnership,which willprovideadditionalmanagement supportfor
watershed and firemanagement.
For an applicationthatincludesacquisitionoffeetitle:Indicatetheintendedholder(s)of
a conservation/agriculturaleasement overtheproperty,orprovidea rationalefor
exemptingtheacquisitionfrom easementrequirements.
An exemption fora conservation/agriculturaleasementwillbe soughtbecauseDLNR/DOFAW
willbe thepropertyowner and willmanage thePohakea WatershedLandsasa forestreserve.

to be conserved,or thatcould be satisfied(wholly or inpart)by a saleoftheproperty.

The applicantforthisprojectwas originallytheMa‘alaeaVillageAssociation(MVA), a 501C3
nonprofitcorporation,formedin2017 afterthedissolutionoftheMa‘alaeaCommunity
Association(MCA). MCA servedMa‘alaearesidentsand businessesfrom 1988,championing
variouscausesincludingharborexpansion,effortstorestoreMa‘alaeaBay andwithfullmember

DLNR/DOFAW with TPLApplicantName (Agency orNonprofit):

support,opposingthedevelopmentof 1100 homes on this257 acreparcel.MVA continuedin
thispursuitjoining effortswith othercommunity groupsSierraClub Maui, Maui Tomorrow,
and Kihei Community Association.MVA leda petitiondrivetopurchasethepropertywith
county open spacefunds,achievinghundredsof signaturesfrom acrosstheisland,which was
presentedtotheMaui County Counciland Mayor’s office.
Concerns focused on thecriticallocationof theparcelattheonly entranceto West Maui,
frequentroad closuresdue totrafficaccidentsand wildfire,urban sprawl,and thepropensityfor
wildfiresabove and through the property.Heightened concerns over sedimentationflowing
through thepropertyto theocean from themountain above proved significantwith the
publicationof MNMRC’s Pohakea Stormwater Management Plan.
MVA sought the supportof Hawaiian IslandsLand Trust(HILT) to facilitatethe purchase.HILT
subsequentlydetermined itdid not have theresourcesto work on theprojectand requested
supportfrom theTrustforPublicLand. With the supportof South Maui Councilmember Kelly
King, funding was allocatedinthe FY 21 budget to initiatethepurchaseprocess;an additional
$5.5millionwas allocatedinFY 22 forthepurchase.This fundingwas supportedby public
testimonyfrom many community organizationsand localbusinessesand a petitionsignedby
over 100 local residents.
MNMRC’s partnershipwith DOFAW to addresswildfireand erosionissuesledtoexploringthe
possibilitythatDOFAW would be willingtoholdtitleto manage thepropertyinperpetuity.
DOFAW has significantexperienceworking withthecommunity to manage the subjectparcel
and surroundingareas.DOFAW’s interestincombiningthe parcelwith the entireWest Maui
watershed and puttingitintoForestryReserve has receivedoverwhelming supportfrom the
community.

Describe the locationof the acquisition,the characteristicsof the land,and the key
resources to be protected (seesubsection 173-5(gl Hawaii RevisedStatutes):
Locaiion and Characiensiics:
Proposed Pohakea preservationparcel((2)3-6-001-018, is257.7 acres of coastalplainslocatedjustab
ove Maui’s Ma'alaea small boat harbor in South Maui. ItisratedALISH “Prime" ag land with deep, well d
rained soils.
Pohakea parcelissurrounded by steeplysloped StateConservation land (3,414 acres)to the South and
West. To the northitisbounded by Pohakea Gulch, an intermittentstream thatflowsfrom KealaloloaRi
dge (3,000 ftamsi) to the KealiaPond nationalWildlifeRefuge.
North of Pohakea gulch are 657 acres of privatelyowned Ag lands thatare partof a perpetualConserve
tion easement dedicated to
Ag use. There isalso a 79 acre privatelyowned rock quarry along Pohakea gulch.The western boundar
y of the 257 acres ends at HonoapiilaniHwy (Rte.31) the main road to Lahaina, one of maui’s major res
oil:locations.
Describe the publicbenefitsof the acquisition,includingbut not limitedto where public
access may be practicableor not practicableand why:
MUDiic benems:
● 257 acre Pohakea Watershed parcel,ifunder publicownership would be partof a “Conservation Corrid
or” in the Ma’alaea, Maui area. This “corridor"would include:Ma’alaea harbor and surrounding reefsand
the the habitatitprovidesforendangered and threatened marine species;nearby KealiaNationalWildlif
e Refuge (Pohakea Gulch passes through the 257 acres and drainsintoKealiawetlands pond;)Various
criticalhabitatareas forrareplantsand animals inthe stateConservationlandsupslope and adjoiningth
e 250 acre;a nene sanctuaryon the ridgeofthe upslopestate lands near Kaheawa Wind farm and the e
xtensivearchaeologicalcomplex immediately adjoiningthe 257 acres.
● Opportunitytojointlymanage high conservationvalue lands adjoiningstateconservationlands.State la
nds have exceptionalhistoricvalue:Ma'alaea Petroglyphsite(Statesite50-50-09-1169); Ma’alaea Com
plex (statesites50-50-09-001/ 50-50-09-1287). Very littlearchaeologicaltestinghas been done on the 2
57 acres, but itislikelythathistoricalremains are present,given the major drainage ways thatintersectt
Describe the context and plan for long-term management of the acquired property:
● In;^ju18,localconservationgroup, Maui Nui Marine Resource Council,commissioned a Pohakea Water
shed Management Plan covering around 5,000 acres of Pokakea watershed land.The plan identifiedthi
s 257 acre coastalplainas a key partof managing down slope stormwater runnoffand restoringthe Agri
culturaland Conservation potentialofthe land.
● In2019, the same group (withsupport lettersfrom variouscommunity partners)secured a $400,000 fe
deralGrant to implement wildfirereductionactivitiesinthe entirePohakea Watershed area,inpartnershi
p with the main landowner, State of Hawaii. The Pohakea Watershed projectmanager sees the 257 -
ere parcelas a key piece inthatimplementation strategy.There isdeep concern thatthe land isunder th
reatof development intoat least20 separate “Agriculturallots,”because the additionof multipleowners
willadd negotiationtime and complexityto any management plans.Publicownership would expeditethe
process.
● Maui Nui has also begun work on an implementationplan forstormwater management inthe entirePoh1 I LU^ lAI 1A/-X-

In order to help the Legacy Land Conservation Commission and the Board of Land and Natural
Resources to process and prioritizeapplicationsforfunding,the Legacy Land ConservationProgram
(LLCP)requeststhata consultingagencycomplete thisform (PartI,and PartIIifapplicable)and return
it—with comments, ifany, on agency letterhead—to the Applicantat the address listedabove, no
laterthan March 30, 2020. Thank youl
The LLCP expects that a consultingagency willbase itsresponses on the Informationpresented in
the attached Form 5 (Informationfrom Land AcquisitionGrant Applicant,includingmaps and
photos).Pleasecontactthe Applicantdirectlywithquestionsregardingthe proposed land acquisition
(Form 5),and contactthe LLCP at 586-0921, or legacyland@hawaii.gov.with questions regarding
agency consultationand the grant process:^	
IftheApplicantdoes not receivean agency response beforeMarch 31,2020 then the applicationwill
indicatethatthe agency "Did Not Respond." Please note thata lackof agency response does
not affectthe prioritizationof an application.
Requestfor ConsultationregardingLand ConservationFund GrantApplication
(Section173A-5,HawaH RevisedStatutes)
Subsection 173A-5(I),Hawaii Revised Statutes(HRS),requiresthatan applicationfora landacquisition
grant from the Land Conservation Fund include“(7)Resultsof the applicant'sconsultationwith the
staffof the department [of land and naturalresources],the department of agriculture,and the
agnbUSiness development corporationregardingthe maximization of public benefits of the
project,where practicable"(emphasis added). The attached Information Form (Form 5)
describesthe proposed landacquisitiongrantforwhich we request agency consultation.
The Legacy Land ConservationProgram (LLCP)suggeststhat a consultingagency consider the
maximization of public benefits withinthe contextof:

itsrecommendations foracquisitions(Section13-140-39.Hawaii Administrative Rules, see
page 18),particularly:

RE; Pohakea Watershed Lands identifiedas TMK: (3) 6-001-018, Wailuku, Island of Maui
Dear Mr. Penn,
MVI. LLC has listedthe subject property for sale. The Pohakea Watershed Lands totals
approximately 257.8 acres. MVI, LLC iswillingto sellthe property to The Trust for Public Land
forsubsequent transferto DLNR/DOFAW, in partnership with the County of Maui and State of
Hawaii, ifwe can reach an agreement as to priceand closing date.
MVI, LLC's preference at Pohakea isto work with The Trust for Public Land, the County of Maui,
the State of Hawaii/DLNR DOFAW.and the various conservation funding programs to preserve
this property.
MVI, LLC tabled moving forward with a 21 lotagriculturalsubdivision we had planned for the
subject property and we recognize the significantpublicbenefitsthat may arise from public
ownership of the Pohakea Watershed Lands property.However, MVI, LLC is sellingallof its
Hawaii land holdings and needs to complete thispurchase in a very timely manner, ftis
imperative that you find purchase funding thisyear,or we will need to seek alternative
purchasers from the public domain.
Sincerely,
Dodglas R. Spehrer

Pohakea Watershed Lands Photos
Photo 1
.X..e
Photo taken from the existingLahaina PaliTrailheadparking area lookingtowards south east property boundary along
Honoapi'ilaniHighway. The property slopesgentlyfrom the West Maui Mountains towards the KealiaPond National
WildlifeRefuge and Ma'alaea Bay.The upland watershed lands are prone to erosion,strong winds and frequent wildfire.
The land lacksadequate stormwater management and silt-ladenrunoff from the mountains sheet flows across it
destroyinghabitatand depositingsiltintoMa'alaea Bay where itsmothers sensitivecoralecosystems.
Photo 2:
Public access to the historic Lahaina Pali Trail isdirectlyoffthe Honoapi'ilaniHighway over the subjectproperty. Given
itscentrallocationand proximity to the most populous areas of Maui, the trailishighlyutilizedand residentstreasure
the easy accessto outdoor recreationand scenicopen space.Thisisa known wildfirearea and unmanaged dried
vegetation and frequent windy conditionscontributeto rapidlyspreading firesthat threaten nearby residencesand
businesses.
The land acquisitionincludesa separate water component which willbenefitthe Pohakea Watershed Land: three
agriculturalwells,a 750,000-gallonstorage tank,and easements forthe deliveryof water across adjacent lands.The
water resources willprovide significantbenefitsforland management and help prevent erosion from damaging marine
environments.
Photo 3:
It-
Aerialview looking south towards the base of the slopes of the iconicWest Maui Mountain and the ocean. Pohakea
Gulch traversesthe property near itsnorthern boundary. The ripariancorridoralong the gulch and nearby areas provide
important habitatfor bats,sphinx moths, and nene. DLNR DOFAW intendsto protectand enhance naturalhabitatand
manage forwatershed restorationby working with volunteersto replantthe ripariancorridorwith lessfireprone
species.The propertyishighlyvisiblefrom Honoapi'ilaniHighway and the threat of residentialdevelopment would mar
the unique aestheticresources of sweeping mauka to makai views as well as destroy habitat,exacerbate erosion,and
potentiallyexpose a greater number of people to firehazards.

NATIONAL MISSION STATEMENT:
The Trust forPublicLand conserves land forpeople toenjoyasparks,gardens,and other
naturalplaces,ensuringlivablecommunitiesforgenerationsto come.
HAWAII MISSION STATEMENT:
Protectingshorelines,heritage,and working landsforthefutureofHawaii'speople.
TPL works with localcommunities and agency partnersinHawai‘itoconserve:(1)
shoreline/coastallands,(2)nativeheritagelandsthatperpetuateHawaiM’s culture,and (3)
working landsthathelpHawai‘ireplaceimports,and leadittoself-sufficiencyinfood,
water,energy,and othergoods,i

GENERAL NOTES
There is hereby omitted from any covenants, conditions and reservations
contained herein any covenant or restriction based on race, color,
religion, sex, sexual orientation, familial status, marital status,
disability, handicap, national origin, ancestry, or source of income,
as set forth in applicable state or federal laws, except to the extent
that said covenant or restriction is permitted by applicable law. Lawful
restrictions under state or federal law on the age of occupants in
senior housing or housing for older persons shall not be construed
as restrictions based on familial status.

GUIDELINES FOR THE ISSUANCE OF INSURANCE
A. Taxes shown in Schedule B are as of the date such information is available from
the taxing authority. Evidence of payment of all taxes and assessments subsequent
to such date must be provided prior to recordation.
B. Evidence of authority regarding the execution of all documents pertaining to
the transaction is required prior to recordation. This includes corporate
resolutions, copies of partnership agreements, powers of attorney and trust
instruments.
C. If an entity (corporation, partnership, limited liability company, etc.) is
not registered in Hawaii, evidence of its formation and existence under the
laws where such entity is formed must be presented prior to recordation.
the following is required:If the transaction involves a construction loan,D.
(1) a letter confirming that there is no construction prior to recordation;
or
(2) if there is such construction, appropriate indemnity agreements, financial
statements and other relevant information from the owner, developer, general
contractor and major sub-contractors must be submitted to the Company for
approval at least one week prior to the anticipated date of recordation.
Forms are available upon request from the Company.
E. Chapter 669, Hawaii Revised Statutes, sets forth acceptable tolerances for
discrepancies in structures or improvements relative to private property
boundaries for various classes of real property. If your survey map shows a
position discrepancy that falls within the tolerances of Chapter 669, call your
title officer as affirmative coverage may be available to insured lenders.
F. The right is reserved to make additional exceptions and/or requirements upon
examination of all documents submitted in connection with this transaction.
G. If a policy of title insurance is issued, it will exclude from coverage all
matters set forth in Schedule B of this report and in the printed Exclusions
from Coverage contained in an ALTA policy or in the Hawaii Standard Owner’s
Different forms may have different exclusions and should
Copies of the policy forms are available upon request from the
Company or on our website at www.tghawaii.com.
Policy, as applicable.
be reviewed.
H. Please be aware that due to the conflict between federal and state laws concerning
the cultivation, distribution, manufacture or sale of marijuana, the Company
is not able to close or insure any transaction involving Land that is associated
with these activities.

Dear Ms. Rothbaum:
SUBJECT: Letter of Support for the Acquisition and Protection
of Ma'aiaea Mauka and Pohakea Watershed (Tax Map
Key (3) 6-001-018) in Ma'aiaea Ahupua'a, Wailuku
Moku, Maui Mokupuni
I am writing to express my support for the proposed acquisition and
protection of Ma'aiaea Mauka and Pohakea Watershed.
As the councilmember for the South Maui residency area which includes
Ma'aiaea, I have seen the consequences of the lack of management of this parcel.
In recent years, wildfires have scorched the mountainside, fueled by dry
vegetation allowed to grow rampantly. Rain events have washed tons of soilinto
Ma'aiaea Harbor and nearby beaches, causing an “impaired” designation for the
bay by the Environmental Protection Agency and State Department of Health in
accordance with the Clean Water Act.
Historic fishing grounds and cultural sites are threatened by ecological
unsustainability and natural hazards. Long-time residents tellstoriesabout this
once healthy and diverse reef ecosystem and express dismay at its decline into
a badly degraded habitat.
The public acquisition and protection of these lands will ensure that
cultural and historic resources are preserved, alleviate runoff problems into the
bay, provide much needed resources for natural hazards mitigation, and enable
the opportunity to expand access to open spaces and the Lahaina PaliTrail.
February 10, 2022
Page 2
Essentially, the acquisition and future management of this property by the
Department of Land and Natural Resources under the Legacy Lands
Conservation Program will ensure protection for perpetuity so this precious
environmental resource can be restored for future generations. I enthusiastically
support this proposal.
Mahalo nui loa.
Maui County Councilmember
Chair, Committee on Climate Action, Resilience & Environment
Member, Local Government Advisory Committee to the U.S. EPA
P. O. Box 2122 Wailuku, Maui, HI 96793
February 10, 2022
Leah Lani Rothbaum
Sustainable Hawaii Project Manager
The Trust for Public Land
1164 Bishop Street, Suite 1512

RE:
Aloha Ms. Rothbaum,
Ma'alaea VillageAssociation(MVA), a 501(c)3Maui nonprofitcorporation,withover190 dues-paying
members, enthusiasticallysupportsthe proposed acquisitionand protectionofMa'alaeaMauka and the
Pohakea Watershed. The protectionofthese landswillensure thatculturaland historicalresourcesare
preserved.Purchase willprovidemuch needed resourcesfornaturalhazardmitigationas wellas the
opportunityto expand access to scenic vistas,open spaces and thecentury-oldLahainaPaliTrail.
The Ma’alaea community, alongwith Maui community groups from KiheitoHaikuand SierraClub Maui
Chapter,has worked fordecades topreserve“Ma'alaeaMauka” Ag. landsatthecrossroadsbetween
Lahaina,Kiheiand CentralMaui, inopen space and freefrom development.The propertywithitsscenic
views of Ma'alaea Bay and Haleakalaiskey to restoringthe Pohakea Watershed.Ma'alaeaBay ishome
forthe Hawaiian IslandsNationalHumpback Whale Marine Sanctuary,and KealiaPond NationalWildlife
Refuge as wellas environmentalleadersMaui Ocean Centerand thePacificWhale Foundation.
Currenteffortsto restoreMa'alaea Bay, designateda 303(d)‘impairedbody ofwaterby EPA, undertaken
by DLNR DivisionofForestryand Wildlife(DOFAW), and Maui nonprofitMaui NuiMarineResource
Council,have begun to address wildfireand erosionthatthreatentheBay and thewatershed.With DLNR
DOFAW planstocombine this257 acre propertywiththe3,000+acreWest Maui watershedinforest
preservewillallowthe stateto manage the entirewatershed,addressingerosionand wildfirechallenges.
MVA and other Maui advocates were successfulinobtainingMaui County CouncilOpen Space funding
toward purchase ofthe property.With TrustforPublicLands helpingtofacilitatetheprocess,we have
made greatheadway withthe County and the landowner towardrealizingthislong-helddream.Legacy
Lands funding could make allthe difference.
We supportthe acquisitionand management ofthispropertyby DLNR DOFAW toensureprotectionand
preservationof thisland’sscenic views,open space and Ma'alaeaBay forfuturegenerationstoenjoy.
Mahalo!
Lynn Britton
Immediate Past President and Co-Founder, Ma'alaea VillageAssociation

Re: Letterof Support forthe Acquisitionand Protectionof Ma'alaeaMauka and Pohakea Watershed {Tax
Map Key (3)6-001-018)inMa'alaeaAhupua'a,Wailuku Moku, Maui Mokupuni
Dear Ms. Rothbaum,
On behalf of our Board of Directors and staff at Maui Nui Marine Resource Council (MNMRC), Iam
writingto express our organization'swholehearted support forthe proposed acquisitionand protection
of Ma'alaea Mauka and Pohakea Watershed by the Stateof Hawaii Department of Land and Natural
Resources Divisionof Forestryand Wildlife.
Since 2018, our nonprofithas focused on identifyingand implementing improvements on these landsto
reduce the sediment runoffthat has plagued Ma'alaea Bay and contributedto the severe declineof its
coral reefs."In 1972, Maalaea coral reefs were described as being 'strikingintheirdiversityand inthe
presence of rarecoralsspecies.'By 1993, estimated coralcover was 50- 75% closeto the sitewhere
cover was measured at 8% in2006." {Hawaii DAR & Hawaii Coral Reef InitiativeResearch Program)
To address the sediment runoffissues,MNMRC publishedthe Pohakea Watershed Stormwater
Management Plan in November 2018, which we had commissioned Maui Environmental Consulting,LLC
to researchand createfor us. The Plan has provided a comprehensive roadmap foraddressingthe
sediment pollutionissuesoriginatingon the landsof Ma'alaea Mauka and Pohakea Watershed.
With thisplan in hand, Maui Nui Marine Resource Councilhas raisedfunding to implement its
recommendations. For example, with support from NationalFish& WildlifeFoundation, we've been able
to installfirebreaksand plantvetivergrassinthe Pohakea Watershed to controlerosion.We've also
completed a monitoring study of three significantheadcuts {erosionalfeatures)on these lands.We
found that during a singlemoderate rainstorm,stormwaters flowing through these headcuts eroded an
estimated 60,000 lbs.of sediment which were likelytransported intoMa'alaea Bay.
The acquisitionand future management of Ma'alaea Mauka and Pohakea Watershed by DLNR DOFAW
willplaya criticalroleinsecuringthe significantresources needed to address these headcuts and control
the runoff.This willultimatelybenefitthe ocean water qualityof Ma'alaea Bay,which isan important
community resource forrecreation,fishingand boating,as well as the coralreefsthat help protectthe
shorelineof Ma'alaea and provide habitatfor marine wildlife.By ensuring thisprotectioninperpetuity,
future generationswillbe able to be able to enjoy the resourcesthat Ma'alaea has to offer.
Sincerely,
Michrof+ogarty
Executive Director
Mike@mauireefs.org
MAUI OCEAN CENTER

wonder and respectforHawaii’smarine life.

Re:
Aloha e Ms. Rothbaum,
Sierra Club Maui Group has accessed this257 acre parcel of land fornearly50 years to offer
educational hikes along the historicLahaina PaliTrail.We are deeply gratefulto the TPL and
the state’sLegacy Lands Conservation Program forconsideringthisiconiclocationfor
permanent preservation forthe people of Hawaii.
As an organization dedicated to environmental education, responsibleoutdoor recreationand
advocacy forour community and nativefloraand fauna, we appreciate the irreplaceablevalue
thisland holds. Itissuch a perfectfitforthe Legacy Lands mission:itprovides habitatforrare
and endangered native species; ithas outstanding scenic views; itisalready surrounded by
publiclands and isthe key piece to Pohakea Watershed management plans to reduce run-off
and sedimentation of our reefs;;itprovides access to and parking forMaui’s mist famous and
popular publictrail(Kealaloloa/ Old Lahaina PaliTrail);itcontains a raretraditionalahupua’a
boundary line(Waikapu-Ukumehame); itisadjacent to the culturallysignificantMa’alaea
petroglyphfieldson stateland;isissome of the best agriculturalland on Maui, with deep
alluvialtopsoils,and ithas been under the threatof massive {900- 1100 units)development for
the last40 years, with only unifiedcitizenaction (and two citizenlegalchallenges) preventing
the various proposals from moving forward.
In short,we must act to keep the naturalbeauty of thisunique place on Maui protectedand well
managed, forour community and futuregenerations to enjoy.
Mahalo nui loa to allconcerned inthiseffortto preserve these worthy lands.

The Trust for Public Land
The Trust forPublicLand has been connectingcommunities totheoutdoors—and toeach
other—since our founding in 1972.To date,we have preservedmore than3.5millionacres,
createdortransformedcloseto2,000parks,playgrounds,and gardens,and developedmore
than 2,000 miles of trailsacross the United States.The TrustforPublicLand has helped
statesand localcommunities generateover $72 billioninnew publicfundsforparksand
open space,includingthe U.S. ForestServiceForestLegacy Program,StateofHawaii
Legacy Land Conservationand CapitalImprovement Programs and countyopen space
funds. In Hawaii, we focus our work on creating,enhancing, and protectingparksand
trails,landsthatperpetuateHawaiianculture,and workinglandsthatcontributetoHawaii’s
self-sufficiency.Community isattheheartofeverythingwe do.
The Trust for PublicLand works in partnershipwith many agencies,organizations,and
communities to conserve land.We contractdirectlywith the landowner via optionsor
purchaseand saleagreementssothatwe have sitecontroland cancompletethenecessary
fundraising,governmentand community engagement,and due diligencetocompletethe
project.Our agencyand non-profittakeoutpartnersrelyon usforourrealestateexpertise
and abilitytotakeactionmore quicklyto meet landowner expectations.Not many people
understandour roleor our work, so we have generallyoutlinedourroleon thefollowing
pages.
TPL As A Primary ContractingParty.TPL isNOT a brokeror realestateagent,
althoughwe usuallyconvey thelandorconservationeasementtofederal,state,andcounty
agenciesand otherNGOs (we calltheseentities“take-out”partners).Our take-outpartners
appreciateour work becausewhile they may be excellentstewardsand managersof
conservationor culturalresources,they oftenlackrealestateand landconservationfunding
expertise,and cannotmove quicklyor obtainappropriategovernmentalapprovalsto
contract with landowners.
ii
Fundraising. TPL leadsallthe fundraisingneeded to successfullycompleteour landprotectionprojects.Inthe
case of the Ma’alaea Mauka, we have alreadyspent significanttime working with community groups and State
of Hawai'i DLNR to discuss community concerns, determine a projectstructure,funding sources,desired
outcomes, and the management plansnecessarydeliverthedesiredoutcomes forthisacquisition.Each funding
source requireswriting detailedresponses to applicationcriteria,providingfmancial/budgetinformation,
researchingthehistoryof the landand itsresources,completingappraisalsand reviewappraisals,and completing
allnecessarydue diligence. Each program has an extensivevettingprocess requiringus to organize/host
numerous sitevisits,present at many public hearings,briefelectedand government officials,meet with
community members and organizations,draftmodel supportivetestimony,and coordinateand submit supportive
testimonybeforevariouscommissions,councils,or decision-makingbodiesor officials.
With regard to federalfunding,we work with our take-outpartnerto craftcompetitivefunding applications,
vettingthesedraftswith our federalaffairsteam in Washington D.C which helpsus improve our applicationsper
federalscoringcriteria.Our federalaffairsteam alsotracksfederalappropriationsto ensurethattheprograms we
relyon are funded at sufficientlevelsand works with our Hawai'i Congressionaldelegationto ensureHawai'i
projectsare funded.
In some cases,we may conduct a privatecapitalcampaign, which requiresmobilizationof our philanthropy
resources- e.g.,creatingmarketing materialsand case statements,meeting with major donors,applying/reporting
to largefoundations,coordinatinggrassrootscommunity appeals,and many more sitevisits.
Fundraisingmillionsof dollarsfrom multiplesourcesforlandprotectiontakestime.Iftheappraisalcomes out
near the value that the current owners have discussed with us,we have identifiedtheMaui Open Space fund as a
potentialsource of the public funding needed, are prepared to move forward to secure the funding, and
expeditiouslyclosethisrealestatetransaction.
Government/Community Engagement. In additionto the officialcommunity and government supportwe
solicitand coordinatefor fundraising,we often facilitateor leadsubstantialcommunity engagement processes
regarding the future visioningand plans for the land. This entailscountless individualcoffee meetings,
community organizationmeetings, and presentationsand updates before various boards, commissions, and
community alliances/huis,as well as coordinationwith our take-outagency or NGO. Itmay requireus to retain
a professionalplanningfirm to assistthe community and takeoutentityin itsvisioningprocess.
We also complete all necessary due diligencesuch as appraisals,review appraisals.Due Diligence,
environmental surveys and clean-ups(subjectto contractualterms),titlereview,and site-inspections.We work
closelywith our take-partnerto addressitsconcerns,and draftdeeds and conservationeasements so thatthey
comply with allof thedifferentfundingsources’requirements.Each fundingsourcerequiresdifferentrestrictions
and may be attemptingto achievedifferentgoals. Coordinatingamong state,county,and federalprograms can
be difficultand time-consuming. However, our attorneyshave been doing thiswork fordecades and are very
skilledat resolvinginter-agencyissues. Each program also has a lengthy listof pre-closingrequirements,
includingforexample, a resourceor baselinedocumentation reportdetailingallof theresourceson the property,
with maps, GIS locations,photos,and descriptions.Many programs requiresubmission of detailedinvoicesand
proof of payment forallcostsrelatedto thetransactiondown to the penny - appraisals,environmentalPhase Is,
titleinsurance,escrow fees,etc.Stateand County programs requiregranteesto submit vendor and taxcompliance
forms. We ensurethatallreports,forms,and burdensome filingrequirementsaremet toenablea timelyclosing.
2
Stewardship. We oftentimessupport our take-outNGOs and even Statetake-outagenciesby includinga
stewardshipendowment or otherinitialstewardshipftmd in our fundraisingeffortsso thatithas the resources to
properlymanage the propertywhen itacquiresthe land.Even ifa government agency isthe takeout,we have
sometimes establishedstewardshipendowments at a community foundationor NGO friendsgroup.
We hope thisclarifiesour roleand our work as we move forward. Pleasefeelfreeto callme or email me ifyou
have any questionsatlea.hong@tpl.org,808-783-3653.

TPL Appraisal Policy
The appraisalisa key element inany TrustforPublicLand (TPL, www.tol.org^assistedconservation
transaction.An appraisalsetsthepropertyvalueforpublicagencies,many ofwhich arelegallyrequiredto offer
fairmarket valueforproperty,and an appraisalassuresthatTPL willnotbe overpayingfora property,and thus
conferringa privatebenefiton a landowner.
ItisinTPL’s, theacquiringagency’sand thepublic’sbestintereststhatappraisalsbe performed by qualified
appraisers,inconformancewithappropriatestandardsand methodologies,and basedupon thebestdata
suggestingvalue,with rigorousreview of thefinalresults.
TPL’s policyistosupporta publicagency appraisalprocessthatisindependent,fairand has integrity.TPL
believesitisimportanttoassurethepublic,and thepublicagenciestowhich we transferlands,thatthey are
acquiringlandfrom TPL fora fairprice.
To implement thispolicy,TPL will:
_ rigorouslyfolloweach agency’sappraisalrulesand procedures;
_ hireonly themost accomplishedappraisers;
_ instructappraiserstoconform tothestandardsidentifiedinthe UniformStandardsofProfessionalAppraisal
Practice,and where applicable,the Uniform AppraisalStandardsfor Federal Land Acquisitions;
_ providetotheAgency, upon itsrequest,any writteninformationinourpossessionthatisrelevanttothevalue
of thesubjectproperty,unlessTPL isprohibitedfrom releasingthisinformationby a confidentialityagreement;
and conform to the highestethicalstandards.

Trust for Public Land Public BenefitsPolicy
Revisedand Restated:September4,2018
The TrustforPublicLand isa nationalnonprofitorganizationthatconservesland forpeople to enjoy as
parks,community gardens,historicsites,rurallands,and othernaturalplaces.Protectingtheplaces
peoplecareabout --from innercitytowilderness— isatthe heartof our work.
The TrustforPublicLand’s work depends on thegeneroussupportof donors and volunteerswho share
our missionof conservingland forpeople.The Wall StreetJournal’sSmartMoney Magazine, Money, Forbes,
and The Chronicleof Philanthropyhave allratedTPL among the most efficientcharitiesin the United Statesfor
keeping fund-raisingand operatingcostslow while meeting missiongoals.
The TrustforPublicLand isa recognized501(c)(3)publiccharityand, as such,donationstoThe Trustfor
Public Land are tax deductible. The Trust for Public Land isnot a membership organizationand receives
no dues.Instead,we receivedonationsand supportfrom corporations,foundationsand individuals-
includinglandowners.We ask landowners to contributeto The Trust forPublicLand invirtuallyevery
transaction. And because landowners well understand our mission,skillsand dedication,we usuallyare
successfulin receivingtheirsupport.
All donationsto The Trust forPublicLand are plowed back intoour work toconservelandforpeople.
Our donors voluntarilysupportThe Trust forPublicLand because of theirinterestinthelandwe protect,
and because of our “Land and People” mission.This supportallowsThe TrustforPublicLand toriskitsown
fundsto protectthreatenedlandsforthepublic,topay fortheconsiderableprojectcoststhatwe incur,and to
generallysupporttheorganizationand our mission.The public,and publicagenciesinparticular,benefitfrom
The TrustforPublicLand’sapproachtofundinglandprotectioninseveralways:
Save cherished lands.
Because we are independentlyfunded,we can takefinancialriskstoprotectextraordinary,and oftenthreatened,
properties- risksthatpublicagenciesareappropriatelyreluctanttotake.As an experiencedorganizationwith
more than fortyfiveyearsof success.The TrustforPublichasparticipatedinmore than4,500transactions
protectingover3,600,000acresoflandvaluedatover$8.2billion.
Allow limitedpublicfunds togo further.
Our goalistocreatesavingsforthepublicby conveyinglandtopublicagenciesforlessthanfairmarketvalue,
the pricethatmany agenciesare requiredto offer. The TrustforPublicLand typicallysavesagenciesproject
costs.We save agencieshundreds of thousandsof dollarseach yearinappraisal,survey,staff,option
considerationand otherprojectcostsby absorbingthem ourselves.Thanks toourdonors,we areabletopay for
coststhatotherwise would be borne by the government agency,and ultimately,by thetaxpayer.

The TrustforPublicLand does not offer(and by thisdocument does notprovide)taxadviceand
makes no representationsas to the tax consequences ofany transactioncontemplatedby this
document. Landowners are encouraged to retainindependent tax advisorsand to relyon their
advice inevaluatingthe tax implicationsof a transaction.
WORKING WITH THE TRUST FOR PUBLIC LAND:
INCOME TAX INFORMATION FOR LANDOWNERS
January 24, 2018
The purpose of thisdocument isto provide landowners sellingor donating land or conservation
easements to The Trust for Public Land with information about:
The tax treatment of cash donations to The Trust for Public Land in connection with a
landowner's saleof property to The Trustfor PublicLand.
IRSsubstantiationrequirements for donations or bargainsalesto The Trustfor Public
Land of land and conservation easements.
Procedures adopted by The Trust for PublicLand when requested to signa landowner's
IRS Form 8283.
The Trust for Public Land welcomes donations of land and conservation easements and cash
donations from landowners who wish to support The Trust for Public Land and our mission.
Many donors may be unfamiliarwith current IRS requirements and failureto properlycomply
could resultinadverse consequences, conceivablyincludinglossof a charitablededuction.
Ifa landowner intends to claim a tax deduction in connection with a real estate transaction with
The Trustfor PublicLand, itisthe landowner's responsibilityto ensure that the transaction
meets the requirements of the InternalRevenue Code Section 170, the accompanying Treasury
Department Regulationsand any other federalor staterequirements.The materialbelow
provides only general guidance.
The rulesregardingtax deductions for giftsof land or conservationeasements can be extremely
complicated.The Trustfor PublicLand iscommitted to working with landowners to help them
obtain availabletax benefits.However, The Trust for PublicLand willnot participatein any
transaction about which we have significantconcerns about a tax deduction.
The Trust for PublicLand always encourages landowners to retain independent tax advisors and
to relyon theiradvice in evaluatingthe tax implicationsof donations and meeting the IRS
requirements forsubstantiationof donations.
1
Cash Donations
Many landowners support The Trustfor PublicLand'scharitablework by making a donation to
The Trustfor PublicLand when theirproperty issoldto The Trustfor PublicLand. A logicalway
of treatingsuch a donation isas a cash giftto The Trust for PublicLand, not unlikeother cash
donations the donor makes to favored charities.
But there may be another way of analyzingthe donation,one that may yielda greaterafter-tax
benefitto the donor. We believeyou should be aware of thispossibility,incase you wish to
discussitwith your advisers.
Itispossibleto take the two elements of the transaction—the saleto The TrustforPublicLand
and the donation to The TrustforPublicLand—and integrateor "collapse"them such that the
substance of the transaction isa sale to The Trust for Public Land at less than fair market value.
A saleof propertyto a charityat lessthan itsfairmarket valueisknown as a "bargainsale".Ina
bargain sale,there are reallytwo transactions:the saleof the propertyto the charityand a
donation to the charityof a portionof the property'svalue.
Let'sstartby usinga sample projectinwhich The TrustforPublicLand ispurchasingproperty
with a fairmarket value of $800,000 from a landowner ata priceof$800,000.The landowner's
basisinthe propertyfortaxpurposes is$40,000.At closing,the landownerismakinga $200,000
cash donation to The Trust for Public Land to support The TrustforPublicLand'scharitablework
throughout America. Forthe purposes of thisexample, itisassumed the landownerisinthe
32% federal tax bracket and the 9.3% state tax bracket.
Ifthetransactionistreatedas an acquisitionat$800,000,followedby a cashdonationof
$200,000 to The Trustfor PublicLand, the landowner willhave a capitalgainstaxliabilityon the
differencebetween the pricepaid by The TrustforPublicLand ($800,000)and the taxbasis
($40,000),or $760,000. Inaddition,the landowner willbe entitledto a charitabledeductionfor
the $200,000 giftto The TrustforPublicLand.
There isanother way of lookingat the transaction.Based on the specificfacts,timingand
documentation of a transaction,itmay be argued thatthe substance ofthe transaction,
integratingthe saleand the donation,isessentiallya saletoThe TrustforPublicLand ata
deemed priceof$600,000 (the$800,000 purchasepricepaidby The TrustforPublicLand less
the $200,000 donation to The TrustforPublicLand).As such,the transactionwould representa
bargain sale as described above.
Viewed inthismanner, the landowner would have capitalgainstaxliabilityforthe difference
between the pricedeemed to be paid by The TrustforPublicLand ($600,000)and the adjusted
basis($30,000—in a bargainsalethe basismust be allocatedbetween thesalecomponent of
the transactionand the giftcomponent) or $570,000. Inaddition,the landownerisentitledto a
charitabletax deduction for the $200,000 of value donated to The TrustforPublicLand.
The bargainsaletreatment givesthe landowner a bettertaxresult(saving$40,515)because
$190,000 lessissubjectto capitalgainstax.
2
While there may be taxadvantages to the integratedbargainsaletreatment,thereare
disadvantagesthatshould be taken intoaccount,when compared tothe cashdeduction
treatment:
● The donation must be substantiated by an appraisalof the donated interestand
substantiated on IRS form 8283, as discussed below.
● Deductions for long-term capitalgain property may not exceed 30% of adjustedgross
income (AGI)for any year,with the unused deduction being carriedforward forfive
years. Deductions for cash donations,incontrast,may not exceed 60% of AGI ina year,
with the unused deduction being carriedforward forfiveyears. Therefore,donors may
be able to take advantage of deductions forcash donations more completelyand
quicklythan isthe case fordonations and bargainsalesof land.(NOTE: Donationsof
conservation easements may enjoy greatertax benefitsthan donationsof land.)
Donors who wish to pursue bargain saletreatment should consultwith theirlegaland financial
advisors and should understand the risk that the IRS might not agree with that characterization.
No two transactions are identical.
NOTE: Unless priorto the conveyance of the property interestthe Donor has advised The Trust
for PublicLand differently,any giftacknowledgment letterprovided by The Trust for PublicLand
willreflectthe understanding thatthe Donor has electednot to pursue bargain saletreatment.
Tax Substantiationfor Property Donations to The Trustfor Public Land
The purpose of thissectionisto describe IRSsubstantiationrequirements forgiftsof land and
conservation easements.
● Donations of Land
A taxpayer must fileForm 8283 with the IRS to claim a tax deduction for a donation of property
exceeding $500 invalue-$5,000 forC corporationsotherthan personalservicecorporationsand
closelyheld corporations.Inaddition,fora donation of propertyor interestinpropertyvalued
over $5,000,the donor must have a "qualifiedappraisal"from a "qualifiedappraiser."Pleasesee
More about Form 8283 and QualifiedAppraisalsbelow.
● Donations of Conservation Easements
A donation of a conservation easement meeting the definitionof a "qualifiedconservation
interest"under Section 170(h) of the InternalRevenue Code and relatedregulationsqualifies
the donor to claim a charitable deduction for the fair market value of the easement. The
requirements discussedabove forfilingForm 8283 and obtainingan appraisalalsoapply to
donations of conservation easements.
3
● Bargain Sales of Land and Conservation Easements
The Form 8283 and appraisalsubstantiationrequirements apply to bargain salesof land or
conservation easements, sincebargain salesinvolvea donation of property value.A bargain sale
isthe conveyance of property to a charityor governmental agency at a pricebelow the
property'sfairmarket value.The landowner isdeemed to make a charitablecontributionof the
discount in price.
● More about IRS Form 8283 and QualifiedAppraisals
As noted above, a taxpayer must fileForm 8283 with the IRSto claima taxdeduction foran
outrightdonation or a bargainsaleof propertyor interestinpropertyexceeding $500 invalue.
Inaddition,fora donation of propertyor interestinpropertyvalued over $5,000,the donor
must obtaina "qualifiedappraisal"from a "qualifiedappraiser"inhand, and the appraisermust
signan appraisalsummary on Form 8283. The appraisalitselfmust be includedwith Form 8283
ifthe fairmarket value of the donation exceeds $500,000 or ifthe donated property isan
easement on a buildinginan historicdistrict.
Itisthe donor's responsibilityto determine the value of any giftof land or a conservation
easement and obtain the needed appraisal.Appraisalrequirements can be complicated,
particularlywith respectto conservation easements and every donor and tax advisorshould
consultthe IRS regulationson appraisalrequirements.The followingare a justa few thingsto
keep in mind:
The appraisalmust be made no sooner than sixty(60)days priorto the date of the
giftand must be received by the taxpayer no laterthan the filingdate (orany
extension)of the income tax return. Ifthe effectivedate of the valuationisafter
the donation, itmust reflectthe value of the conservationeasement or fee property
on the date of the gift.
The Trustfor PublicLand stronglyencourages donors to obtain high quality
appraisalsfrom qualifiedappraiserswho are well-positionedto appraisethe subject
property and followthe Uniform Standards of ProfessionalAppraisalStandards.
Characteristicsof a "qualifiedappraiser"are discussedinthe IRS Instructionsfor
Form 8283
Omission of any of the IRS required appraisalelements. Includingwithout limitation,
thatappraisalstake intoaccount any enhancement invalueto the donor'sretained
property resultingfrom the conservation donation, may disqualifythe donor from
claiminga charitablededuction.
The appraisalmust statethat itwas prepared for income tax deduction purposes.
o
o
o
o
The Trust for Public Land willrequest a copy of the appraisalsupporting any IRS Form 8283 itis
asked to sign.
Donors should also be aware that the IRS isexercisingincreasedscrutinyon the valuationof
property interests,includingconservationeasements, donated to charities.Thisresults,inpart,
from documented cases of abusive valuations.
4
Additionally,fordonations of conservationeasements, a statement must be attachedto the
Form 8283 that:
Identifiesthe conservation purposes of the donation.
Shows the fairmarket value of the property before and afterthe gift,ifthat method
of valuation was used.
States whether the donation was made in order to obtain a permit or other
approval from government and whether the donation was required by contract.
Describesany interestof the taxpayer or a relatedparty ina nearby property.

● The Trust for Public Land Practices:
The Trustfor PublicLand has adopted the followingprocedures and practicesregardingits
review of IRS Forms 8283:
Any IRS Form 8283 sent to The Trustfor PublicLand for signingmust be completed
and signed by both the landowner and appraiser.Itmust alsoincludethe related
appraisaland any other requiredinformation,such as the supplemental statement
required forconservation easement.
o
The Trust for Public Land will review the Form 8283 and the accompanying materials
and share any concerns with the landowner. We willwork with the landowner to
resolve any issuesand may request additionalsubstantiationof value ifnecessary.
o
The Trust for PublicLand typicallyneeds at least30 days to review any Form 8283
and appraisal.Please plan accordingly.
o
The Trust for PublicLand may refused to sign a Form 8283 ifwe believethat we have not
receivedthe giftdescribed on the Form 8283 or ifwe have any other significantconcerns about
the tax deduction.
NOTE: The Trust for PublicLand isrequired to inform the IRS ifwithin three years of a giftfor
which we signed a Form 8283 we sell,exchange or transferthe property (orany portion of the
property) by flingIRS Form 8282. Insuch a case,we willsend a copy of the Form 8282 to the
donor.
Donors should consult with theirtax advisors, or review the following IRS publicationsfor more
information:
Publication 526: Charitable Contributions
Publication561: Determining the Value of Donated Property
Instructions for Form 8283

parks likenever before - for fresh air,exercise,

from the stressesof a quickly changing world.

to-home parks and natural areas are crucialto a
community’s quality of lifeand public health.

economy, create green jobs, and increase local
food production because job and food security

in ‘AinaHaina,0‘ahu.When the propertywas

ralliedto preserve the natural land forpublic

agreement will preserve numerous culturalsites
and ensure the property isused foragriculture
in perpetuity.The Ala Kahakai TrailAssociation
holds thiseasement to safeguard culturalsites
and perpetuate Ka'u’s paniolo traditionsand local
grass-fedbeef production.
The Trust for Public Land works in partnership
with communities across Hawai’i to conserve
the lands they love.We work with landowners
to protecttheirland forpublic benefit,identify
funders who believein our land-for-people
mission, and partner with communities to bring
theirvisionfora property’sfutureto life.
Supporters likeyou make thistransformational

We purchased the 3,443-acreKamehamenui

in Kula, Maui, and conveyed itto the Hawai‘i
Department of Land and Natural Resources,
Division of Forestryand Wildlife (DLNR). DLNR

and open the formerly privateproperty to the

Reforestationwillrecharge the water aquiferto
ensure clean drinking water for the futureand to
protect endangered species.

Celebrating the Great American
Outdoors Act
Thanks to decades of tirelessadvocacy from a
diverse coalitionof park lovers across the country
and Hawaii’s Congressional delegation,we’re
celebratingan incrediblelegislativevictory:
permanent, full,and dedicated funding for LWCF.
We recentlysought additionalLWCF funding
that vidllallow us to complete the protection
of Mapulehu on Moloka’i,and our Hawai‘1 Koa
Forest and Pohue Bay conservation projects on
Hawai’i Island.
At the federal, state,and local levels,The Trust
for Public Land advocates for the importance
of parks and open space and the need to fund
them. Securing permanent, full,and dedicated
funding forthe Land and Water Conservation
Fund (LWCF) has been the top legislativepriority
for The Trust for Public Land for over 30 years.A
lotgoes intomaking a law thisbig happen - and
our Federal Affairs team in D.C. has been at the
front leading the charge.This longstanding federal
program directsfeesfrom oiland gas drilling
to investments in parks and open space.Itcosts
taxpayers nothing-but benefitseveryone.
The Senate introduced the Great American
Outdoors Act in earlyMarch with two main goals:
to fixthe longstanding $9.5billionmaintenance
backlog in our nationalparks,forests,and other
publiclands:and to guarantee fulland dedicated
funding - $900 millionevery singleyear - for
LWCF. The billpassed the Senate inJune, passed
the House of Representatives in July,and on
August 4 the billwas signed into law. HAWAI I KOA FOREST

ishome to Tli‘ili‘6paeHeiau, the second largest

About The Trust for Public Land
The Trust for Public Land has been connecting communities to the outdoors—and to each other—
sinceour founding in 1972. To date,we have preservedmore than 3.5 millionacres,createdor
transformedcloseto 2,000 parks,playgrounds,and gardens,and developed more than 2,000 miles
of trails across the United States. The Trust for Public Land has also helped statesand local
communities generateover $72 billioninnew publicfunds forparks and open space,includingall
of the Stateand County land conservationprograms in Hawai'i.
Our work rum thegamut ofprotectingnationalparks to neighborhoodparks,conservingspecial
places thatcommunities loveand cherish.In Hawai'i,we’ve protectedover 54,000 acresof land in
partnershipwith federal,state,and county agencies,and otherNGOs. We’ve helped theNational
Park Serviceexpand Volcanoes NationalPark,HaleakalaNational Park,Pu'uhonua O Honaunau
National HistoricPark,and partsof the Ala Kahakai National HistoricTrail.And, we have helped
community organizationsliketheNorth Shore Community Land Trust protectshorelineand rural
landscapesincludingover 1,000acressurroundingTurtleBay Resort,Waimea Valley,Pupukea-
PaumalO, and Sunset Ranch - allforcommunity benefit.In December 2019, The Trust forPublic
Land helpedthe Stateof Hawai'iDepartment of Land and NaturalResources acquire3,716 acres
at Waimea Native Forest- the upper prioritywatershed of Waimea Valley.
Hawai^i Program Overview
The Trust for Public Land createsparks and protectsland for people to ensure healthy,livable
communities for generationsto come. We believethat equity and fairnessmatter,and that a
relationshipbetween people and natureiscriticalforhealthypeople and a healthylandscape.Our
visionforHawai‘i isthatitspeople are thriving,healthy,and meaningfully connected to the land,
each other,and a livingHawaiian culture.The Trust for Public Land accomplishesthisvision
through our strategiesof Parks forPeople,SustainableHawaii, and Aloha ‘Aina.We’ve protected
over 54,000 acresthroughoutthe Stateinpartnershipwith many agenciesand NGOs.
Parks for People:At theTrustforPublicLand, we believethateveryone shouldhave a park,
playground,trail,and othernaturalplaceswithina 10-minutewalk from home. Every day,we see
thatwhen childrenhave thechance to learnfrom and play innature,when neighborhoods have safe
community gatheringspace to enjoy the outdoors,and when neighbors of differentbackgrounds
buildcommunity bonds - everybody wins. In late2020 we willlauncha new urban parksprogram
with a pilotprojectat ‘A‘ala Park in Downtown Honolulu/Chinatown, a very diverse and
underservedcommunity. We willleada community participatoryvisioningand planningprocessin
which surroundingresidentsand businessescome togetherto articulatea sharedvisionforthe Park.
Together we can createa safe,welcoming ‘A‘alaPark for the children,families,seniors,and the
generalpublicwho liveand work inthe area.
2
In 2019, the TrustforPublicLand assistedthe City & County |
of Honolulu in acquiringa 9-acre property to expand the
Wailupe Nature Preserve.The land is withina residential
community and providespublicaccess intoWailupe Valley
for hiking,culturalpractices,and managing elepaiocritical
habitaton the watershed land.TPL secured funding for the

In 2017, the Trust for Public Land worked with nonprofit
Livable Hawai‘1 Kai Hui and the broader community to
purchasethe lastthreatenedparcelsof landalong the Ka Iwi
Coast on 0‘ahu. The protectionof these landshonored 40
years of grassrootsadvocacy that saved the surrounding
coastlineasopen space.These mauka landsaresurroundedby
the denselypopulatedHawaii Kai community and belovedfor
theirnativeplants,culturalsites,and thescenicviewplanes.
Sustainable HawaiT: The Trust for Public Land’s vision for Hawai‘i is ‘aina momona- islands
thatare self-sufficientand abundant, with the abilityto feed and nourishour peopleand provide
ample cleanwater forthe future.Although Hawai‘i was once self-sufficient,feedinga population
estimatedatone million,we now import90% ofourfood.As islands,ourwaterresourcesarelimited
to what isgeneratedinour aquifers,streams,and springs- yethalfof Hawaii’s originalwatershed
forestshave been destroyed.By working with privatelandownersto conserveagriculturaland
forestedlands,opportunitiesarecreatedforfarmersto own and leaselandataffordablepricesand
forlongerterms,increaseHawaii’s food securityand self-sufficiency,and diversifyoureconomy.
In 2019, we helped the Wai‘anae Community Redevelopment
Corporation,dba MA‘0 Organic Farms, to expand in two
separateprojects.The firstwas a 21-acre parcelMA“0 is
alreadygrowing fruitsand vegetableson, with an estimated
outputof 12,000pounds of food permonth. The second project^
was a 236-acre property,100 of which will be farmed and ^
another 40 acres will be used to develop affordablefarm
manager and internhousing.TPL providedtechnicalassistance
to MA‘0 in the second project,includingfacilitatinga $1.1 ^
million contributionby the U.S. Navy through itsbuffer
program forthisproject.
In 2019, we partneredwith the Stateof Hawai‘i DLNR’s
Divisionof Forestryand Wildlife(DOFAW) to protect3,716 |
acresof the upper mauka landsof Waimea Valley known as
Waimea Native Forest.TPL securedfullfunding fortheS3.7M g
purchasethroughfivesources,and transferredownershipto
DOFAW, protectingpreciousnativerainforest,endangered
birds,plants,snailsand bats,and high prioritywatershed
securingcleandrinkingwater forgenerationstocome.

Aloha ‘Aina: The TrustforPublicLand’s organizationalcommitment to equity,health,and
community isreflectedinourAloha ‘Ainaprogram.‘Aina,theHawaiianword for‘land’,means that
which feeds. ‘Aina is foundational to Native Hawaiian culture,identity,and traditionalpractices.
Connection to land is essentialto the physical,emotional and spiritualwell-beingof Native
Hawaiians. Likewise,the healthof theseplacesistiedto the healthof Hawaiian communities. The
concept of aloha ‘aina,or love of the land,teachesus thatifyou take care of the land,itwilltake
care of you. Yet the historyof Hawai‘i has involved catastrophiclossof land.Culturalsitesand
burialshave been destroyed or segmented, Hawaiians have been displaced from theirlands,and
traditionalcustoms and practiceshave been severed and lost.The Trust for Public Land works in
partnershipwith Native Hawaiian communities to protectland from development and returnthese
landsto Hawaiian organizationsforcommunity-based stewardship,empowering allin Hawai‘i to
aloha ‘aina.
In 2019, TPL partnered with the Ala Kahakai Trail
Association(ATA) to purchase Waikapuna, a 2,317-acre
propertyinKa‘u thatisrichinculturalheritagesites,habitat®
fornestingseabirds,humpback whales,and turtles,aswell b
as prime grazing land for cattle.Hundreds of ancient
culturalsitesaredocumented on theproperty,includingthe
petroglyphsand konane board featuredin thisphoto.TPL
secured the full $6M for the purchase and transferredWlS
ownership to ATA.
^ 3
E >●
Also in2019, TPL partneredwith Kuahiwi Ranch toplace
an conservation easement owned by ATA over 772 acres
in Ka‘u known as Kawala. Kawala is nestled between
Waikapuna and Kaunamano, and is a combination of
sprawlingpastureland,numerous lavatubes,culturalsites,| .^7
and ancientburials.The land isowned by Kuahiwi Ranch
and the easement restrictsthe land to agriculturaland
culturalpreservationuses in perpetuityand protects
importanthistoricalsites.

What we do & why
The TrustforPublicLand createsparks and protectsland forpeople,ensuringhealthylivablecommunities for
generationstocome. We believethatequityand fairnessmatter,and thata relationshipbetween peopleand natureis
criticalforhealthypeople and a healthylandscape.
In Hawai'i, health is the foundation of our work. We focuson meaningfullyconnectingpeople to theland and each
other,and support theempowerment of underserved and NativeHawaiian communities.Our visionisthatthepeople
of Hawai'i arethrivingand healthy,and meaningfullyconnectedtotheland,eachother,and a livingHawaiian culture.
The TrustforPublicLand accomplishesthisvisionthroughour strategiesofParksforPeople,SustainableHawai'i,and
Aloha 'Aina.

As Owners of a short-term vacation rentalplease see our comments below, thank you.
Short term vacationrentalscan offerunique experiencesnothing likethe commercial resorthotelscan offer.It's
important that people understand that there ismore to itthan justlettingpeople stay inour home to make money. It
involveskindness,politeness,caringof residentneighbors,respectforrulesas wellas localpeople and cultures,and
gratitudewhich isallthe best form of humanity. As owners, itisour opportunity and responsibilityto promote itin
order to contributetowards community healthand growth.
Bylaws and regulationscould weed out owners who are not prepared to adhere to bylaws or rules.We already heard
from our guestsworrying about not being able to stayin our condo anymore. Insteadof banning alltogether,could you
please lookforthe ways to save the qualityof shortterm rentalsinMaui?
Thanks to the short term vacation rentalopportunity,itwas 30 years into our marriage when we were finallyable to
explore Maui for the firsttime. Itwas the most memorable vacation. There was a guest book inthe condo which we
ended up readingthe entirebook filledwith joy and happiness from pasttravelersfrom allover the world. This isthe
reason why we started our own vacation rentalin Maui with a hope and passion to share the beauty and our loveof
Maui with many others.
Instead of the fruitplatterat the hotel,our guests explore localfarmers markets to enjoy locallygrown produce. Instead
of lunch or cocktailsserved by the pool,our guestsexplore localrestaurants,food trucks,and happy hours. Insteadof
dining out at on-siteresorthotel restaurants,our guests explorelocalstoresto cook with localfreshingredients.We
activelytake a great partof promoting localbusinesses.We now have many repeated guests,and some ofthem even
get involved in the localvolunteer activitiesbecause they grow to care about communities.
Dave &Toshiko Jager

mi OCT21 AH 8=08INSTITUTE OF HAWAII
OFFICE OF THF
COUNTY GLEKK
Oct. 21. 2022
9 a.m.
To: Council of the County of Maui
Alice Lee, Chair
Keani Rawlins-Fernandez, Vice Chair
From: Grassroot Institute of Hawaii
Joe Kent, Executive Vice President
Comments Only
Dear Chair and Committee Members:
The Grassroot Institute of Hawaii would like to offer its comments on proposed resolution
22-206 — “Authorizingproceedings ineminent domain forthe acquisitionof approximately 257
acres at Ma'alaea Mauka/Pohakea Watershed, Maui, Hawaii.”
We have concerns thatthisproposal would needlessly infringeprivateproperty rightsand set a
dangerous precedent of using eminent domain to appease voters who oppose particular
proposed residentialdevelopments.
The resolutionstatesthatitspurpose isto use the Ma'alea Mauka/Pohake Watershed for
"watershed projects,open space, reservoirdevelopment and publicparks.”
But italso admits that “therehas been publicsupport for Maui County to acquire thisland in an
effortto curb additionaldevelopment,” which suggests the proposed acquisitionismore about
politicsthan protectingthe environment.
Considering the severityofthe county’shousing shortage,thistrulywould be a strange policy
forthe county to adopt — buying land to prevent needed homes from being built.
As we allknow, Maui already has among the most burdensome housing regulationsinthe
nation.The misuse of eminent domain to furtherdiscourage new homebuilding would add yet
another element of uncertaintyto the homebuilding process.
Italso would impose considerable unneeded costs on Maui taxpayers, who already are
stretched to the limitto pay the county’s bills,as well as theirown.
Even ifwe take the alleged environmental goals ofthisresolutionat face value, itisnot obvious
thatthe land could not also accommodate the buildingof new homes — which, again, is
something Maui residentsdesperately need.
Additionallythere islittlereason to believethe county can address the environmental needs of
thisarea betterthan the privatesector.As with most government bodies, itsenvironmental track
record isless than encouraging, and we often read about neglected or damaged publicpark
restrooms, loitering and dumping, abandoned cars, and persistentlitter,on county properties.
In conclusion,even when supposedly used forthe “publicgood,” eminent domain isnonetheless
an infringementon privatepropertyrightsand should not be used frivolously,such as to
appease “Not In My Backyard" groups opposed to new housing.
To do so would put the County inthe awkward positionof buying land to prevent housing from
being built,at a time when itshould be encouraging homebuilders instead.
We think itwould be wise for the Council to shelve thisproposed resolution.
Sincerely,
Joe Kent
Executive Vice President
Grassroot Institute of Hawaii

with our property rights.Itdoes not appear thatthe economic ramificationsofthisordinance have

itssize and upgrading it.No bedrooms or bathrooms were added. Next year we are lookingatrepainting
the interiorand installingsome new flooring.These kindsofimprovements resultindollarsbeing spent in
the localMaui economy on materialsand labor.This high levelofinvestmentispossiblebecause thereis
a volume of visitorsthatare willingto pay forqualityaccommodations. This resultsinsignificantreal
propertytax and transienttax revenues to Maui County, inadditionto visitorspending from these
guests. We are opposed to Bill159 and the provisionthatchanges TransientVacationRentalsinplanned
developments from permitted,to non-conforming. The potentialnegativeeconomic impacttothe island
economy could be significant.
Bryce Klug
